# 7.4 Human Approval & Editorial Gates

# Part 7.4.1 — Purpose & Editorial Authority Boundary

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section defines the purpose, scope, and boundary of **Human Approval & Editorial Gates**: the points in the pipeline where a human editorial decision is required, and the strict separation between that human judgment and the mechanical enforcement the gates perform.

Sections 7.1 through 7.3 defined mechanical gates: deterministic, fail-closed checks and scores that a machine evaluates. This section defines the complementary layer where a human being makes a judgment the machine cannot: whether an artifact is editorially fit to advance.

The platform's mechanical gates answer "is this well-formed and admissible?" A human editorial gate answers a question no check can: "is this *right* — does it say what we intend, and are we willing to stand behind it?" This section defines where that human judgment enters, how it is recorded, and how it is kept distinct from the machinery that enforces its presence.

---

# Governing Principle

The platform adopts the following principle.

> **A human editorial gate records a human judgment; a mechanical gate enforces that the judgment exists. The human owns the substance of the decision; the machine owns only its presence, scope, and currency. Neither performs the other's role.**

This is the separation established in 7.1.1 (enforcement is not editorial judgment) and 7.2.10 (the Publication Gate confirms approval presence, never self-approves), now defined as its own layer. The editorial authority decides; the mechanical gate checks that a decision was made, applies to this exact artifact, and has not lapsed.

---

# Three Distinct Authorities

The platform separates three authorities over an artifact.

```text
Narrative Authority   → owns what the story means        (Part 2, upstream, immutable)
Editorial Authority   → owns whether to approve & publish (this section)
Enforcement Authority → owns mechanical gate verdicts     (7.1, mechanical)
```

* **Narrative Authority** settles story meaning upstream; it is immutable once the Story Gate passes (Part 4.1.1).
* **Editorial Authority** decides whether an artifact is editorially fit to advance and to be published.
* **Enforcement Authority** is the mechanical gate engine; it holds no discretion (7.1.10).

Editorial Authority is distinct from both. It does not reopen narrative meaning, and it does not perform mechanical enforcement. It exercises human judgment at defined approval points and records that judgment.

---

# The Human Owns Substance, the Machine Owns Presence

The division of labor between human and machine is exact.

```text
Editorial Authority (human)          Mechanical Gate (machine)
  ├── decides: approve / reject         ├── checks: does an approval exist?
  ├── judges editorial fitness          ├── checks: does it match this identity?
  └── records justification             └── checks: is it current (not expired/revoked)?
```

The human makes the decision and records it. The machine confirms the decision exists, applies to the exact artifact identity, and remains current. The machine never makes the decision, and the human never overrides a mechanical verdict.

This is why the Publication Gate's `editorial_approval_recorded` check (7.2.10) is mechanical: it checks *presence*, and presence is a mechanical fact. The *approval itself* is a human judgment made here.

---

# Approval Is a Formal Dependency

An editorial approval is a formal dependency in the build, not an informal process note.

This generalizes the storyboard approval boundary of Part 4.1.1: *approval is represented as a formal dependency, not an informal process note. No rendering may proceed until storyboard approval is recorded.*

An approval is:

* a recorded artifact with an identity and a scope;
* an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency in the dependency model (Part 6.3.6);
* a precondition the mechanical gate enforces as a check.

An artifact that requires approval and lacks it is blocked by the mechanical gate exactly as a failed mandatory check blocks it (7.1.5). Approval is a first-class, recorded precondition, never a verbal or out-of-band sign-off.

---

# The Editorial Gate Boundary

The editorial layer is bounded by two contracts.

```text
Upstream Contract
Artifact + Artifact Identity + Quality Report (7.3)
        │
        ▼
Editorial Authority (human judgment)
        │
        ▼
Downstream Contract
Recorded Approval Decision (bound to identity, scoped, timestamped)
```

The upstream contract gives the human what they need to decide: the artifact, its identity, and its mechanical quality report (7.3.6). The downstream contract is the recorded decision, bound to the exact artifact identity.

The human consumes mechanical evidence and produces a recorded judgment. The mechanical gates then enforce that judgment's presence downstream.

---

# What the Editorial Layer Does Not Do

The editorial layer is bounded by explicit non-scope.

## Mechanical Enforcement

The editorial authority never performs a mechanical gate's job. It does not evaluate checks, compute scores, or render machine verdicts. A human cannot "pass" an artifact that failed a mandatory check by editorial fiat — a mandatory FAIL blocks regardless of approval (7.1.5).

---

## Narrative Reinterpretation

The editorial authority never reopens settled story meaning. Approval is a decision to advance or publish, not a licence to rewrite the premise, protagonist, or arc, which are immutable upstream (Part 4.1.1).

---

## Waiving the Non-Waivable

The editorial authority never releases a non-waivable check (7.1.10). An editorial approval cannot override a child-safety or rights failure at the Publication Gate (7.2.10). Approval operates above the non-waivable floor, never through it.

---

# Approval Does Not Replace Mechanical Gates

An editorial approval is additive to the mechanical gates, never a substitute.

```text
Mechanical gates PASS  AND  editorial approval recorded  →  advance
Mechanical gates FAIL  →  blocked, regardless of approval
```

An approved artifact that fails a mandatory check is still blocked. Approval is required *in addition to* passing the mechanical gates; it never rescues a mechanical failure. This mirrors exactly the relationship between scoring and the mandatory floor (7.3.1): the editorial layer adds a condition, never removes one.

---

# Anti-Patterns

## AP-971 — Editorial Override of a Mandatory FAIL

Wrong:

```text
mandatory check FAILs → editor approves anyway → advance
```

Correct:

```text
mandatory FAIL blocks; approval cannot rescue it
```

---

## AP-972 — The Machine Self-Approving

Wrong:

```text
gate marks the artifact approved on the editor's behalf
```

Correct:

```text
approval is a recorded human decision; the gate only checks its presence
```

---

## AP-973 — The Verbal Sign-Off

Wrong:

```text
editor verbally approves; nothing recorded
```

Correct:

```text
approval is a recorded, scoped, timestamped dependency
```

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant EAB-701

Editorial Authority is distinct from Narrative Authority and Enforcement Authority.

---

## Invariant EAB-702

A human editorial gate records a human judgment; a mechanical gate enforces that the judgment exists.

---

## Invariant EAB-703

The human owns the substance of an approval; the machine owns only its presence, scope, and currency.

---

## Invariant EAB-704

An editorial approval is a formal, recorded dependency, never an informal sign-off.

---

## Invariant EAB-705

An approval is bound to the exact artifact identity it was granted for.

---

## Invariant EAB-706

The editorial authority never performs mechanical enforcement.

---

## Invariant EAB-707

The editorial authority never reopens settled narrative meaning.

---

## Invariant EAB-708

An editorial approval never releases a non-waivable check.

---

## Invariant EAB-709

Approval is additive to the mechanical gates and never rescues a mandatory FAIL.

---

## Invariant EAB-710

The machine never self-approves and the human never overrides a mechanical verdict.

---

# Architectural Decision Records

| ADR      | Decision                                                                            |
| -------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ADR-1014 | Editorial Authority is distinct from Narrative and Enforcement Authority.            |
| ADR-1015 | A human editorial gate records judgment; a mechanical gate enforces its presence.    |
| ADR-1016 | The human owns approval substance; the machine owns its presence, scope, and currency. |
| ADR-1017 | An editorial approval is a formal, recorded dependency.                              |
| ADR-1018 | An approval is bound to the exact artifact identity.                                 |
| ADR-1019 | The editorial authority never performs mechanical enforcement.                       |
| ADR-1020 | The editorial authority never reopens narrative meaning.                             |
| ADR-1021 | An editorial approval never releases a non-waivable check.                           |
| ADR-1022 | Approval is additive and never rescues a mandatory FAIL.                             |
| ADR-1023 | The machine never self-approves; the human never overrides a mechanical verdict.      |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.4.1** is complete when:

* The human approval layer is defined and its purpose distinguished from mechanical gating.
* Narrative, Editorial, and Enforcement authorities are separated.
* The division of labor — human owns substance, machine owns presence — is defined.
* Approval is established as a formal, recorded dependency.
* The editorial gate boundary contracts are defined.
* Non-scope — mechanical enforcement, narrative reinterpretation, waiving the non-waivable — is established.
* Approval is shown additive to, never a substitute for, mechanical gates.
* Anti-patterns for editorial override, self-approval, and verbal sign-off are documented.
* Invariants EAB-701 through EAB-710 and ADR-1014 through ADR-1023 are established.

---

# Part 7.4.2 — Approval State Model

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section defines the **Approval State Model**: the states an approval moves through, the transitions between them, and the binding of an approval to an artifact identity.

Section 7.4.1 established that an approval is a formal, recorded dependency. This section defines its lifecycle: from not-yet-requested, through review, to a terminal decision, and the conditions under which a granted approval is voided.

An approval is a stateful object. A mechanical gate checking `editorial_approval_recorded` (7.2.10) is really checking that an approval for this artifact identity is in the `APPROVED` state and current. This section defines those states precisely so that check is unambiguous.

---

# Design Philosophy

The platform adopts the following principle.

> **An approval is a state machine bound to an artifact identity. Its terminal decision is explicit and recorded, and any change to the artifact identity voids a granted approval.**

Two commitments follow.

An approval is **identity-bound**: it applies to exactly one artifact identity (7.1.5). An approval for one identity is meaningless for another.

An approval is **explicitly terminal**: it reaches a recorded decision — approved, rejected — and does not linger in ambiguity. Absence of a decision is not implicit approval; it is the absence of approval, which blocks.

---

# The Approval States

An approval moves through the following canonical states.

```yaml
NOT_REQUESTED

REQUESTED

UNDER_REVIEW

APPROVED

REJECTED

REVOKED

EXPIRED
```

* **NOT_REQUESTED** — no approval has been requested for this artifact.
* **REQUESTED** — approval has been requested and is pending review.
* **UNDER_REVIEW** — an editorial authority is actively reviewing.
* **APPROVED** — the editorial authority has approved this artifact identity.
* **REJECTED** — the editorial authority has rejected this artifact identity.
* **REVOKED** — a previously granted approval has been withdrawn.
* **EXPIRED** — a previously granted approval has lapsed by time.

---

# The Approval State Diagram

```text
NOT_REQUESTED
   │  approval requested
   ▼
REQUESTED
   │  review begins
   ▼
UNDER_REVIEW ──── rejected ──► REJECTED
   │  approved
   ▼
APPROVED ──── revoked ──► REVOKED
   │  │
   │  └──── expired ──► EXPIRED
   │  artifact identity changes
   ▼
(approval voided → NOT_REQUESTED for the new identity)
```

Every path to advancement passes through `APPROVED`. `REJECTED`, `REVOKED`, and `EXPIRED` are non-advancing states. A change to the artifact identity voids any approval and returns the new identity to `NOT_REQUESTED`.

---

# State Definitions

## NOT_REQUESTED

No approval exists for this artifact identity.

A mechanical gate requiring approval treats `NOT_REQUESTED` as a FAIL: no approval is present. This is the default state; approval is never implicit.

---

## REQUESTED

Approval has been requested and awaits review.

The artifact, its identity, and its quality report (7.3.6) are made available to the editorial authority. A `REQUESTED` approval is pending, not granted; a gate treats it as not-yet-approved.

---

## UNDER_REVIEW

An editorial authority is actively reviewing the artifact.

`UNDER_REVIEW` is informational; it records that review is in progress. A gate treats it as not-yet-approved.

---

## APPROVED

The editorial authority has approved this exact artifact identity.

`APPROVED` is the only advancing state. It records the approver, the artifact identity, the scope, a justification, and a timestamp and expiry. A gate requiring approval passes its approval check only when the approval is `APPROVED` and current.

---

## REJECTED

The editorial authority has rejected this artifact identity.

`REJECTED` records the approver, the identity, and a justification. A rejected artifact is returned for correction; the corrected artifact has a new identity and begins at `NOT_REQUESTED`.

---

## REVOKED

A previously granted approval has been withdrawn.

Revocation (7.4.5) moves an `APPROVED` approval to `REVOKED`. A revoked approval no longer satisfies a gate; the artifact is blocked until re-approved. Revocation is recorded with its reason and authority.

---

## EXPIRED

A previously granted approval has lapsed by its expiry.

An `APPROVED` approval carries an expiry; once passed, it becomes `EXPIRED`. An expired approval no longer satisfies a gate. Expiry ensures a stale approval cannot indefinitely authorize an artifact that may no longer be appropriate to publish.

---

# Terminal and Non-Advancing States

The states divide by their effect on advancement.

| State           | Advances? | Notes                                          |
| --------------- | --------- | ---------------------------------------------- |
| NOT_REQUESTED   | No        | No approval present.                           |
| REQUESTED       | No        | Pending review.                                |
| UNDER_REVIEW    | No        | Review in progress.                            |
| APPROVED        | **Yes**   | The only advancing state, while current.       |
| REJECTED        | No        | Returned for correction.                       |
| REVOKED         | No        | Withdrawn; re-approval required.               |
| EXPIRED         | No        | Lapsed; re-approval required.                  |

Only `APPROVED`, and only while current, advances. Every other state blocks.

---

# Approval Binding to Artifact Identity

An approval is bound to exactly one artifact identity.

```text
approval APPROVED  @  artifact_identity = sha256(...)
```

If the artifact changes by a single byte, its identity changes (7.1.5), and the approval no longer applies. The changed artifact is at `NOT_REQUESTED` and must be approved anew. This is the state-model basis for the `approval_scope_matches` check at the Publication Gate (7.2.10): an approval for a prior identity does not cover a changed trailer.

Identity binding is what prevents the most dangerous approval failure: publishing a trailer that was altered after it was approved.

---

# Approval Is Never Implicit

Absence of a decision is never approval.

```text
Wrong:  no rejection recorded → treat as approved
Correct: no APPROVED state → not approved → blocked
```

An approval must be an explicit, recorded `APPROVED` decision. Silence, timeout, or absence of rejection is not approval. This is the fail-closed model (7.1.5) applied to the approval layer: the default is not-approved, and approval is earned only by an affirmative recorded decision.

---

# Anti-Patterns

## AP-981 — Implicit Approval by Silence

Wrong:

```text
no one rejected it → treat as approved
```

Correct:

```text
only an explicit APPROVED state advances; silence blocks
```

---

## AP-982 — Approval Surviving an Edit

Wrong:

```text
artifact edited after approval → keep the APPROVED state
```

Correct:

```text
identity changes → approval voided → new identity at NOT_REQUESTED
```

---

## AP-983 — The Immortal Approval

Wrong:

```text
approval granted once → valid forever
```

Correct:

```text
approval carries an expiry; a lapsed approval is EXPIRED and blocks
```

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant ASM-701

An approval moves through the states NOT_REQUESTED, REQUESTED, UNDER_REVIEW, APPROVED, REJECTED, REVOKED, EXPIRED.

---

## Invariant ASM-702

APPROVED, while current, is the only advancing state.

---

## Invariant ASM-703

An approval is bound to exactly one artifact identity.

---

## Invariant ASM-704

A change to the artifact identity voids any approval and returns the new identity to NOT_REQUESTED.

---

## Invariant ASM-705

Approval is never implicit; absence of a decision is not-approved.

---

## Invariant ASM-706

An APPROVED approval records approver, identity, scope, justification, timestamp, and expiry.

---

## Invariant ASM-707

A REJECTED approval records approver, identity, and justification.

---

## Invariant ASM-708

A REVOKED or EXPIRED approval no longer satisfies a gate.

---

## Invariant ASM-709

An expired approval cannot indefinitely authorize an artifact.

---

## Invariant ASM-710

A mechanical gate's approval check passes only for an APPROVED, current approval bound to the exact artifact identity.

---

# Architectural Decision Records

| ADR      | Decision                                                                            |
| -------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ADR-1024 | An approval is a state machine over seven canonical states.                          |
| ADR-1025 | APPROVED, while current, is the only advancing state.                                |
| ADR-1026 | An approval is bound to exactly one artifact identity.                               |
| ADR-1027 | A change to the artifact identity voids any approval.                                |
| ADR-1028 | Approval is never implicit; the default is not-approved.                             |
| ADR-1029 | An APPROVED approval records approver, identity, scope, justification, and expiry.    |
| ADR-1030 | REVOKED and EXPIRED approvals no longer satisfy a gate.                              |
| ADR-1031 | Approvals expire so a stale approval cannot indefinitely authorize an artifact.       |
| ADR-1032 | A gate's approval check passes only for a current APPROVED approval on the exact identity. |
| ADR-1033 | Rejected artifacts are corrected and re-enter approval at NOT_REQUESTED under a new identity. |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.4.2** is complete when:

* The seven approval states are defined with their meanings.
* The approval state diagram is specified, with APPROVED as the only advancing state.
* Each state's effect on advancement is tabulated.
* Approval binding to a single artifact identity is established and tied to `approval_scope_matches`.
* Implicit approval is prohibited; the default is not-approved.
* Expiry and revocation are shown to void a granted approval.
* Anti-patterns for implicit approval, approval surviving an edit, and immortal approvals are documented.
* Invariants ASM-701 through ASM-710 and ADR-1024 through ADR-1033 are established.

---

# Part 7.4.3 — Editorial Gate Types

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section defines the **editorial gates** the platform runs: the specific points in the pipeline where a human approval is required, and the artifact each approves.

Section 7.4.2 defined the approval state model. This section defines where in the pipeline approvals are required, mapping each editorial gate to its stage, its artifact, and the mechanical gate that enforces its presence.

The platform requires human approval at a small number of high-consequence boundaries. This section enumerates them so the pipeline's human decision points are explicit and complete.

---

# Design Philosophy

The platform adopts the following principle.

> **Human approval is required at the fewest, highest-consequence boundaries: where cost is about to escalate sharply, and where output is about to become irreversible. Each editorial gate approves one artifact and is enforced by one mechanical gate.**

Human approval is scarce and deliberate. It is placed where a machine cannot decide and where the consequence of proceeding is large: before expensive rendering, and before irreversible publication. The platform does not require approval at every boundary — only where human judgment is genuinely load-bearing.

---

# The Editorial Gates

The platform defines the following editorial gates.

| Editorial Gate            | Approves                          | Before                        | Enforced by            |
| ------------------------- | --------------------------------- | ----------------------------- | ---------------------- |
| Storyboard Approval Gate  | The approved storyboard           | Render execution (expensive)  | Render Gate (7.2.6) dep |
| Preview Approval Gate     | The preview build                 | Final build                   | Final build precondition|
| Final Approval Gate       | The final trailer                 | Packaging / publication       | Publication Gate (7.2.10)|
| Publication Approval Gate | Release to a specific target      | Public release                | Publication Gate (7.2.10)|

Each editorial gate approves exactly one artifact and is enforced by a mechanical gate that checks the approval's presence, scope, and currency (7.4.2).

---

# Editorial Gate Type

Every editorial gate is, in the classification of 7.1.6, a human-approval gate.

```yaml
gate_type:
  scope:    (varies)          # element or sequence
  trigger:  stage-exit
  blocking: blocking
  judgment: human-approval
```

An editorial gate's mechanical counterpart has judgment type `human-approval`: it enforces the *presence* of a recorded human decision. The human decision itself is the editorial gate; the mechanical check is its enforcement. The two are paired, exactly as 7.4.1 established.

---

# The Storyboard Approval Gate

The Storyboard Approval Gate is the platform's primary cost-control approval.

```text
Storyboard passes Storyboard Quality Gate (7.2.5)
        │
        ▼
Storyboard Approval Gate  ◄── human approves the storyboard
        │  APPROVED
        ▼
Render execution may proceed (expensive)
```

This generalizes the storyboard approval boundary of Part 4.1.1: *no rendering may proceed until storyboard approval is recorded.* Approval here is the human decision that the storyboard is worth the cost of rendering. It is placed exactly where cost is about to escalate sharply — before the expensive, non-deterministic render stage.

The mechanical Render Gate (7.2.6) and the render–storyboard coherence gate (7.2.11) depend on this approval being recorded and current.

This is a direct application of the platform principle that expensive computation is deferred until human approval (Part 6.1 — Human Approval Before High-Cost Work).

---

# The Preview and Final Approval Gates

Preview and Final Approval Gates govern the two build products.

The platform produces preview and final builds as independent products (Part 6). Each has an approval gate.

* **Preview Approval Gate** — a human approves the preview build before a final build is authorized. The preview is cheap and iterable (Part 4.1.10); its approval is the decision to commit to a final render.
* **Final Approval Gate** — a human approves the final trailer before it may be packaged and published. This is the last editorial judgment on the trailer's content.

Both are human-approval gates bound to their build's artifact identity (7.4.2).

---

# The Publication Approval Gate

The Publication Approval Gate is the platform's irreversibility control.

```text
Final trailer packaged (passes Packaging Gate, 7.2.9)
        │
        ▼
Publication Approval Gate  ◄── human approves release to a target
        │  APPROVED
        ▼
Publication Gate (7.2.10) enforces approval + rights + safety
```

Publication is irreversible: a released trailer has reached an audience. The Publication Approval Gate is the human decision to accept that irreversibility for a specific target. It is enforced by the Publication Gate's `editorial_approval_recorded` and `approval_scope_matches` checks (7.2.10).

The Publication Approval Gate operates *above* the non-waivable floor: it authorizes release only after rights and safety have mechanically passed. An approval never releases a non-waivable failure (7.4.1, EAB-708).

---

# Editorial Gates Are Placed by Consequence

The placement of editorial gates follows a single logic.

```text
Approval required where:
   ├── cost is about to escalate sharply  → Storyboard Approval (before render)
   └── output is about to become irreversible → Publication Approval (before release)
```

Between these, Preview and Final approvals mark the commitment from cheap iteration to expensive final and from final to publishable. The platform does not place an approval at every stage boundary — most boundaries are mechanically decidable. Approvals are reserved for the boundaries where human judgment is genuinely required and consequential.

---

# Each Editorial Gate Approves Exactly One Artifact

An editorial gate approves one artifact identity, not a class of artifacts.

```text
Storyboard Approval  → approves storyboard identity X
Final Approval       → approves final trailer identity Y
Publication Approval → approves release of identity Y to target Z
```

A single approval never covers "all storyboards" or "this and future versions." It is bound to one artifact identity (7.4.2), so a changed artifact requires a fresh approval. This prevents a blanket approval from authorizing content the approver never saw.

---

# Anti-Patterns

## AP-991 — Rendering Before Storyboard Approval

Wrong:

```text
storyboard passes mechanical gate → render immediately, approve later
```

Correct:

```text
Storyboard Approval recorded before expensive render proceeds
```

---

## AP-992 — Blanket Approval

Wrong:

```text
approve "this storyboard and any minor revisions"
```

Correct:

```text
approve exactly one artifact identity; revisions require fresh approval
```

---

## AP-993 — Approval Placed Everywhere

Wrong:

```text
require human approval at every stage boundary
```

Correct:

```text
approvals only where cost escalates or output becomes irreversible
```

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant EGT-701

The platform defines editorial gates at the storyboard, preview, final, and publication boundaries.

---

## Invariant EGT-702

Every editorial gate's mechanical counterpart has judgment type human-approval.

---

## Invariant EGT-703

The Storyboard Approval Gate is required before expensive render execution.

---

## Invariant EGT-704

The Publication Approval Gate is required before irreversible release.

---

## Invariant EGT-705

Editorial gates are placed only where cost escalates sharply or output becomes irreversible.

---

## Invariant EGT-706

Each editorial gate approves exactly one artifact identity.

---

## Invariant EGT-707

A single approval never covers a class of artifacts or future versions.

---

## Invariant EGT-708

The Publication Approval Gate operates above the non-waivable floor and never releases a non-waivable failure.

---

## Invariant EGT-709

Each editorial gate is enforced by a specific mechanical gate that checks approval presence, scope, and currency.

---

## Invariant EGT-710

The Storyboard Approval Gate realizes the Part 4.1.1 and Part 6.1 human-approval-before-high-cost boundary.

---

# Architectural Decision Records

| ADR      | Decision                                                                            |
| -------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ADR-1034 | Editorial gates exist at the storyboard, preview, final, and publication boundaries. |
| ADR-1035 | Every editorial gate is enforced by a human-approval mechanical gate.                |
| ADR-1036 | Storyboard approval is required before expensive render execution.                   |
| ADR-1037 | Publication approval is required before irreversible release.                        |
| ADR-1038 | Editorial gates are placed only at high-consequence boundaries.                      |
| ADR-1039 | Each editorial gate approves exactly one artifact identity.                          |
| ADR-1040 | No approval covers a class of artifacts or future versions.                          |
| ADR-1041 | Publication approval operates above the non-waivable floor.                          |
| ADR-1042 | Each editorial gate is enforced by a specific mechanical gate.                        |
| ADR-1043 | The Storyboard Approval Gate realizes the human-approval-before-high-cost boundary.   |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.4.3** is complete when:

* The editorial gates — storyboard, preview, final, publication — are enumerated with their artifacts and enforcing gates.
* The human-approval gate type is established for each.
* The Storyboard Approval Gate is defined as the cost-control approval before rendering.
* The Publication Approval Gate is defined as the irreversibility control before release.
* The placement logic — cost escalation and irreversibility — is defined.
* Each editorial gate is shown to approve exactly one artifact identity.
* Anti-patterns for rendering before approval, blanket approval, and over-placement are documented.
* Invariants EGT-701 through EGT-710 and ADR-1034 through ADR-1043 are established.

---

# Part 7.4.4 — Approval Dependencies & Scope

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section defines how an approval participates in the build as a **dependency**, and how its **scope** is bounded to an exact artifact identity.

Section 7.4.3 defined where approvals are required. This section defines how an approval is wired into the build's dependency model (Part 6.3.6) and how its scope is defined so that it authorizes exactly what the approver saw and nothing more.

An approval is only useful if the build cannot proceed without it and if it authorizes precisely the artifact it was granted for. This section makes both guarantees precise.

---

# Design Philosophy

The platform adopts the following principle.

> **An approval is an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency whose scope is exactly one artifact identity. The dependent work is blocked until the approval is satisfied, and the approval authorizes nothing beyond its scoped identity.**

Two commitments follow.

An approval is a **hard dependency**: dependent work does not run until the approval is `APPROVED` and current. This is enforced through the dependency model, not through convention.

An approval is **exactly scoped**: it authorizes one artifact identity. Anything outside that scope — a changed artifact, a different target — is not authorized.

---

# Approval as an APPROVAL_REQUIRED Dependency

An approval is modeled as an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency (Part 6.3.6).

```text
Render tasks
        │
        │ APPROVAL_REQUIRED
        ▼
Storyboard Approval (must be APPROVED, current)
```

The dependent tasks — render, package, publish — declare an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency on the relevant approval. The Orchestrator's readiness evaluation (Part 6.3.6) treats the dependency as satisfied only when the approval is in the `APPROVED` state and current (7.4.2). An unsatisfied approval dependency blocks the dependent tasks exactly as any unmet dependency does.

This wires human approval into the same dependency machinery that governs all task readiness, so approval is enforced structurally rather than by process discipline.

---

# Approval Satisfaction States

The `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency maps the approval state to a satisfaction state.

| Approval state  | Dependency satisfaction |
| --------------- | ----------------------- |
| NOT_REQUESTED   | UNSATISFIED             |
| REQUESTED       | WAITING                 |
| UNDER_REVIEW    | WAITING                 |
| APPROVED (current) | SATISFIED            |
| APPROVED (expired) | UNSATISFIED          |
| REJECTED        | FAILED                  |
| REVOKED         | UNSATISFIED             |
| EXPIRED         | UNSATISFIED             |

Only a current `APPROVED` approval satisfies the dependency. `REJECTED` fails the dependency, propagating a failure to dependent tasks. Every other state leaves the dependency unsatisfied or waiting, blocking the dependent work.

---

# Approval Scope

An approval's scope defines exactly what it authorizes.

```yaml
approval_scope:
  artifact_identity: sha256:...    # the exact artifact approved
  gate: storyboard_approval_gate   # which editorial gate
  target: null                     # for publication approvals, the specific target
  build_generation: 4              # the build generation approved
```

The scope binds the approval to an artifact identity, an editorial gate, and — for publication — a specific target. An approval authorizes exactly this scope and nothing outside it. A different artifact identity, a different target, or a different build generation is outside scope and unauthorized.

---

# Scope Matching

A mechanical gate confirms the approval scope matches the artifact being processed.

```text
artifact being published: identity Y, target Z
approval scope:           identity Y, target Z
        │
        ▼
scope matches → approval satisfies the gate

approval scope:           identity Y, target W
        │
        ▼
scope mismatch → approval does NOT satisfy the gate
```

This is the `approval_scope_matches` check of the Publication Gate (7.2.10). An approval granted for target W does not authorize release to target Z; an approval for identity Y does not authorize a changed identity Y'. Scope matching is exact and mechanical.

---

# Scope Is Never Widened

An approval's scope is never broadened after the fact.

```text
Wrong:  approval for identity Y → reused for identity Y' after an edit
Correct: identity Y' is out of scope → requires a fresh approval
```

Scope is fixed at the moment of approval and bound to what the approver saw. It is never widened to cover a changed artifact, an additional target, or a later build generation. Widening scope would authorize content the approver never reviewed, which defeats the purpose of approval.

This is the scope-level expression of the identity binding established in 7.4.2 (ASM-703) and the `approval_scope_matches` check in 7.2.10.

---

# Approval Dependencies Compose With Mechanical Gates

An approval dependency and the mechanical gates are both required.

```text
Dependent work runs ⟺ mechanical gates PASS  AND  approval dependency SATISFIED
```

The two are conjunctive. An artifact that passes every mechanical gate but lacks a satisfied approval is blocked. An artifact with a satisfied approval but a failed mechanical gate is blocked. Both conditions must hold — approval never substitutes for a mechanical pass (7.4.1, EAB-709).

---

# Anti-Patterns

## AP-1001 — Proceeding on an Unsatisfied Approval

Wrong:

```text
approval REQUESTED (not yet APPROVED) → start rendering
```

Correct:

```text
APPROVAL_REQUIRED unsatisfied → render blocked until APPROVED
```

---

## AP-1002 — Reusing an Approval Across Scope

Wrong:

```text
approval for target Z → publish to target W under the same approval
```

Correct:

```text
scope mismatch → target W requires its own approval
```

---

## AP-1003 — Widening Scope After an Edit

Wrong:

```text
artifact edited → extend the old approval to the new identity
```

Correct:

```text
new identity is out of scope → fresh approval required
```

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant ADS-701

An approval is modeled as an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency in the build dependency model.

---

## Invariant ADS-702

The approval dependency is satisfied only by a current `APPROVED` approval.

---

## Invariant ADS-703

A `REJECTED` approval fails the dependency and propagates failure to dependent tasks.

---

## Invariant ADS-704

An approval's scope binds an artifact identity, an editorial gate, and, for publication, a target.

---

## Invariant ADS-705

A mechanical gate confirms the approval scope matches the artifact being processed.

---

## Invariant ADS-706

An approval authorizes exactly its scope and nothing outside it.

---

## Invariant ADS-707

Approval scope is never widened after the fact.

---

## Invariant ADS-708

A changed artifact identity, target, or build generation is outside an existing approval's scope.

---

## Invariant ADS-709

Dependent work runs only when mechanical gates pass and the approval dependency is satisfied.

---

## Invariant ADS-710

An approval never substitutes for a mechanical gate pass.

---

# Architectural Decision Records

| ADR      | Decision                                                                            |
| -------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ADR-1044 | An approval is an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency in the build dependency model.        |
| ADR-1045 | The approval dependency is satisfied only by a current APPROVED approval.             |
| ADR-1046 | A REJECTED approval fails the dependency and propagates failure.                      |
| ADR-1047 | An approval's scope binds identity, editorial gate, and (for publication) target.     |
| ADR-1048 | A mechanical gate confirms scope matches the artifact being processed.                |
| ADR-1049 | An approval authorizes exactly its scope and nothing outside it.                      |
| ADR-1050 | Approval scope is never widened after the fact.                                      |
| ADR-1051 | A changed identity, target, or generation is out of an existing approval's scope.     |
| ADR-1052 | Dependent work requires both mechanical passes and a satisfied approval dependency.    |
| ADR-1053 | An approval never substitutes for a mechanical gate pass.                            |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.4.4** is complete when:

* An approval is defined as an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency in the Part 6.3.6 model.
* The mapping from approval state to dependency satisfaction is tabulated.
* Approval scope binding identity, gate, and target is defined.
* Scope matching is defined and tied to `approval_scope_matches` (7.2.10).
* The prohibition on widening scope is established.
* The conjunction of approval and mechanical gates is defined.
* Anti-patterns for unsatisfied-approval proceed, cross-scope reuse, and scope widening are documented.
* Invariants ADS-701 through ADS-710 and ADR-1044 through ADR-1053 are established.

---

# Part 7.4.5 — Approval Revocation & Re-Approval

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section defines **revocation** — the withdrawal of a granted approval — and **re-approval** — the granting of a fresh approval after revocation, expiry, or artifact change.

Section 7.4.2 established that a granted approval can move to `REVOKED` or `EXPIRED`, and that an artifact change voids an approval. This section defines the mechanics: who may revoke, what revocation does to in-flight work, and how re-approval restores a build to a proceedable state.

Revocation is the editorial authority's ability to say "stop" after having said "go." Re-approval is the path back to "go." Both must be precise, recorded, and safe against races with in-flight work.

---

# Design Philosophy

The platform adopts the following principle.

> **A granted approval can be revoked by the editorial authority at any time before its authorized work completes. Revocation immediately voids the approval and blocks dependent work that has not yet committed an irreversible action. Re-approval is a fresh approval, never a reinstatement of the old one.**

Two commitments follow.

Revocation is **immediate**: once revoked, an approval no longer satisfies any dependency, and dependent work that can still be stopped is stopped.

Re-approval is **fresh**: it is a new approval decision on the current artifact identity, recorded anew. A revoked or expired approval is never "un-revoked" or "renewed" in place; the editorial authority makes a new decision.

---

# Revocation

Revocation withdraws a granted approval.

```text
APPROVED
   │  editorial authority revokes
   ▼
REVOKED  (approval no longer satisfies any dependency)
```

Revocation is performed by the editorial authority that holds approval power (7.4.1). It records the revoking authority, the artifact identity, a justification, and a timestamp. Once revoked, the approval's `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency (7.4.4) becomes unsatisfied, and dependent work is blocked.

Revocation is the editorial safety valve: if new information makes a previously approved artifact inappropriate, the approval can be withdrawn before its authorized work completes.

---

# Revocation and In-Flight Work

Revocation interacts with work already in progress under the approval.

```text
Approval REVOKED
        │
        ├── dependent work not yet started  → blocked (dependency unsatisfied)
        ├── dependent work in progress, reversible → cancelled (Part 6.3.15)
        └── dependent work already irreversible (published) → cannot be un-done
```

Revocation stops what can still be stopped. Work not yet started is blocked. Reversible in-progress work — a render in flight — is cancelled through the Orchestrator's cancellation model (Part 6.3.15). Work that has already completed irreversibly — a publication — cannot be undone by revocation; this is precisely why the Publication Approval Gate (7.4.3) exists, to make the irreversible decision deliberately.

Revocation is therefore most powerful before the irreversible boundary and is the reason expensive and irreversible actions are gated behind approval in the first place.

---

# Revocation Before Irreversibility

The value of revocation is bounded by irreversibility.

```text
Storyboard Approval → revocable before render completes → saves render cost
Publication Approval → revocable before release completes → prevents release
                     → NOT revocable after release        → already public
```

An approval is meaningfully revocable only up to the point its authorized action becomes irreversible. This is the editorial-layer reason the platform places approvals immediately before cost escalation and irreversibility (7.4.3): those are exactly the points where a late revocation still has value.

---

# Artifact Change Voids Approval

A change to the approved artifact voids its approval, independently of revocation.

```text
APPROVED @ identity Y
        │  artifact edited → identity Y'
        ▼
approval for Y no longer applies to Y'  (7.4.2, ASM-704)
        │
        ▼
Y' is at NOT_REQUESTED → requires re-approval
```

This is not revocation — no one withdrew the approval — but the effect on advancement is the same: the changed artifact is unapproved. Identity binding (7.4.2) guarantees that an edit after approval cannot silently carry the old approval forward.

---

# Re-Approval

Re-approval is a fresh approval decision.

```text
REVOKED / EXPIRED / voided-by-edit
        │
        ▼
current artifact identity at NOT_REQUESTED
        │  approval requested anew
        ▼
REQUESTED → UNDER_REVIEW → APPROVED (fresh)
```

Re-approval runs the full approval state machine (7.4.2) again on the current artifact identity. It is a new decision by the editorial authority, recorded as a new approval with its own justification, timestamp, and expiry. The revoked or expired approval remains in the record as history; it is never reactivated.

Re-approval never inherits the prior approval's standing. The editorial authority reviews the current artifact and decides afresh.

---

# Re-Approval Is Not Reinstatement

A revoked or expired approval is never reinstated.

```text
Wrong:  un-revoke the old approval → restore APPROVED
Correct: create a fresh approval on the current identity
```

Reinstating an old approval would restore a decision that was deliberately withdrawn or that lapsed, without a fresh review. The platform requires a new decision, so that the editorial authority always approves the current artifact on current information. The old approval's history is retained (7.4.6); it is never resurrected.

---

# Anti-Patterns

## AP-1011 — Un-Revoking an Approval

Wrong:

```text
approval revoked in error → un-revoke to restore APPROVED
```

Correct:

```text
create a fresh approval; the revoked one stays in history
```

---

## AP-1012 — Ignoring Revocation for In-Flight Work

Wrong:

```text
approval revoked → let the in-flight render finish anyway
```

Correct:

```text
revocation cancels reversible in-flight work (Part 6.3.15)
```

---

## AP-1013 — Carrying Approval Across an Edit

Wrong:

```text
artifact edited after approval → keep the approval
```

Correct:

```text
edit voids the approval; re-approval required on the new identity
```

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant ARR-701

A granted approval can be revoked by the editorial authority before its authorized work completes.

---

## Invariant ARR-702

Revocation immediately voids the approval and unsatisfies its dependency.

---

## Invariant ARR-703

Revocation records the revoking authority, artifact identity, justification, and timestamp.

---

## Invariant ARR-704

Revocation blocks not-started work and cancels reversible in-progress work.

---

## Invariant ARR-705

Revocation cannot undo an already-irreversible action such as a completed publication.

---

## Invariant ARR-706

A change to the approved artifact voids its approval independently of revocation.

---

## Invariant ARR-707

Re-approval runs the full approval state machine afresh on the current artifact identity.

---

## Invariant ARR-708

A revoked or expired approval is never reinstated; re-approval is a new decision.

---

## Invariant ARR-709

A prior approval's history is retained and never resurrected.

---

## Invariant ARR-710

Re-approval never inherits the standing of a prior approval.

---

# Architectural Decision Records

| ADR      | Decision                                                                            |
| -------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ADR-1054 | A granted approval can be revoked before its authorized work completes.               |
| ADR-1055 | Revocation immediately voids the approval and unsatisfies its dependency.             |
| ADR-1056 | Revocation records authority, identity, justification, and timestamp.                 |
| ADR-1057 | Revocation blocks not-started work and cancels reversible in-progress work.           |
| ADR-1058 | Revocation cannot undo an irreversible completed action.                              |
| ADR-1059 | An artifact change voids its approval independently of revocation.                    |
| ADR-1060 | Re-approval runs the full state machine afresh on the current identity.               |
| ADR-1061 | A revoked or expired approval is never reinstated.                                    |
| ADR-1062 | A prior approval's history is retained and never resurrected.                         |
| ADR-1063 | Re-approval never inherits a prior approval's standing.                              |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.4.5** is complete when:

* Revocation is defined as the editorial authority's withdrawal of a granted approval.
* Revocation's effect on not-started, reversible, and irreversible work is defined.
* The bound of revocation's value at the irreversibility boundary is established.
* Artifact-change voiding is distinguished from revocation.
* Re-approval is defined as a fresh decision on the current identity.
* The prohibition on reinstatement is established, with history retained.
* Anti-patterns for un-revoking, ignoring revocation, and carrying approval across an edit are documented.
* Invariants ARR-701 through ARR-710 and ADR-1054 through ADR-1063 are established.

# Part 7.4.6 — Approval Audit Log & Traceability

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section defines the **Approval Audit Log**: the append-only record of every approval decision, and the traceability it provides.

Sections 7.4.1 through 7.4.5 defined how approvals are made, scoped, and revoked. This section defines how every one of those actions is recorded, so that the full editorial history of any published trailer is reconstructable long after release.

An approval that leaves no record is indistinguishable from no approval at all. The audit log is what makes editorial accountability real: it records who approved what, when, on what evidence, and under what scope — permanently.

---

# Design Philosophy

The platform adopts the following principle.

> **Every approval action is recorded in an append-only audit log: request, review, approval, rejection, revocation, and expiry. The log is immutable, attributed, and sufficient to reconstruct the complete editorial history of any artifact.**

Two commitments follow.

The log is **append-only**: entries are added, never edited or deleted. The editorial history is a permanent, tamper-evident record.

The log is **sufficient**: it records enough — actor, action, identity, scope, justification, timestamp — to fully reconstruct who decided what and why, without reference to anything outside the log.

---

# The Audit Log Is Part of Governance

The Approval Audit Log is a specialization of the governance audit log (7.1.10).

Editorial approvals are governance actions: they are decisions by an authority that affect what the platform produces and publishes. They are recorded in the same append-only, immutable governance audit log that records waivers and configuration changes (7.1.10). The Approval Audit Log is the editorial view of that record.

This unifies editorial accountability with the platform's overall governance accountability: a single, immutable record captures every discretionary decision — mechanical waiver and human approval alike.

---

# The Audit Log Entry

Every approval action is recorded as an append-only entry.

```json
{
  "action": "approval_granted",
  "editorial_gate": "storyboard_approval_gate",
  "authority": "editorial_lead",
  "artifact_identity": "sha256:storyboard-...",
  "scope": { "identity": "sha256:storyboard-...", "build_generation": 4 },
  "justification": "Storyboard realizes the approved arc; approved for render.",
  "quality_report_ref": "build-quality-2026-07-20-001",
  "decision": "APPROVED",
  "expiry_ref": "build_clock:+72h",
  "recorded_at_ref": "build_clock:tick-6102"
}
```

The entry records the action, the editorial gate, the deciding authority, the artifact identity, the scope, the justification, a reference to the quality report the decision was based on, the decision, the expiry, and a build-clock timestamp.

---

# Every Action Is Logged

The audit log records every approval-related action, not only grants.

| Action              | Recorded |
| ------------------- | -------- |
| Approval requested  | Yes      |
| Review started      | Yes      |
| Approval granted    | Yes      |
| Approval rejected   | Yes      |
| Approval revoked    | Yes      |
| Approval expired    | Yes      |
| Re-approval granted | Yes      |

Rejections, revocations, and expiries are recorded as fully as grants. The log records the complete decision history, including the decisions that blocked advancement, so an audit can see not only what was approved but what was rejected or withdrawn and why.

---

# The Decision Is Tied to Its Evidence

Each approval entry references the quality evidence the decision was based on.

```json
"quality_report_ref": "build-quality-2026-07-20-001"
```

The entry references the Build Quality Report (7.3.6) that was available to the approver at decision time. This ties the human judgment to the mechanical evidence that informed it: an audit can see not only that the artifact was approved, but what its measured quality was when the human approved it.

This linkage is what makes editorial decisions reviewable in context rather than in isolation.

---

# Traceability of a Published Trailer

The audit log makes a published trailer's editorial history fully reconstructable.

```text
Published trailer identity
        │
        ▼
Publication Approval entry      (who authorized release, to what target, when)
        │
        ▼
Final Approval entry            (who approved the final trailer)
        │
        ▼
Storyboard Approval entry       (who approved the storyboard for render)
        │
        ▼
Complete editorial chain
```

For any published trailer, the log yields the complete chain of editorial decisions that authorized it: every approval, its authority, its justification, and the evidence it rested on. A trailer can never be published without leaving this trail, because publication requires a recorded approval (7.2.10) and every approval is logged.

---

# The Log Is Immutable and Retained

The audit log is immutable and retained for the audit horizon.

Entries are never edited or deleted (7.1.10). The log is retained for at least the audit horizon defined by governance, because a published trailer must remain editorially explainable long after release. An approval decision made today is reconstructable years from now, exactly as recorded.

Immutability makes the log tamper-evident: the editorial history cannot be quietly rewritten after the fact.

---

# Anti-Patterns

## AP-1021 — The Unlogged Approval

Wrong:

```text
editor approves in a meeting; nothing recorded
```

Correct:

```text
every approval is an append-only audit entry with authority, scope, and justification
```

---

## AP-1022 — Editing the Log

Wrong:

```text
revise a past approval entry to change its justification
```

Correct:

```text
the log is append-only; a correction is a new entry, never an edit
```

---

## AP-1023 — Approval Without Evidence Reference

Wrong:

```text
approval entry records no link to the quality evidence
```

Correct:

```text
each approval references the Build Quality Report it was based on
```

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant AAL-701

Every approval action is recorded in an append-only audit log.

---

## Invariant AAL-702

The Approval Audit Log is a specialization of the governance audit log (7.1.10).

---

## Invariant AAL-703

Every entry records action, editorial gate, authority, artifact identity, scope, justification, decision, and timestamp.

---

## Invariant AAL-704

Requests, reviews, grants, rejections, revocations, and expiries are all recorded.

---

## Invariant AAL-705

Each approval entry references the quality evidence the decision was based on.

---

## Invariant AAL-706

The log makes a published trailer's complete editorial history reconstructable.

---

## Invariant AAL-707

The log is immutable; entries are never edited or deleted.

---

## Invariant AAL-708

A correction is a new entry, never an edit of a prior one.

---

## Invariant AAL-709

The log is retained for at least the governance audit horizon.

---

## Invariant AAL-710

No trailer is published without a complete, logged editorial approval chain.

---

# Architectural Decision Records

| ADR      | Decision                                                                            |
| -------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ADR-1064 | Every approval action is recorded in an append-only audit log.                       |
| ADR-1065 | The Approval Audit Log is part of the governance audit log.                          |
| ADR-1066 | Every entry records action, gate, authority, identity, scope, justification, and time. |
| ADR-1067 | Requests, reviews, grants, rejections, revocations, and expiries are all logged.      |
| ADR-1068 | Each approval references the quality evidence it was based on.                        |
| ADR-1069 | The log makes a published trailer's editorial history reconstructable.                |
| ADR-1070 | The log is immutable; corrections are new entries.                                   |
| ADR-1071 | The log is retained for the governance audit horizon.                                |
| ADR-1072 | No trailer is published without a complete logged approval chain.                    |
| ADR-1073 | Editorial accountability is unified with governance accountability in one log.         |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.4.6** is complete when:

* The Approval Audit Log is defined as an append-only record of every approval action.
* It is established as a specialization of the governance audit log (7.1.10).
* The audit log entry schema is specified.
* Every approval-related action, including rejections and revocations, is shown to be logged.
* The linkage of each decision to its quality evidence is defined.
* Full traceability of a published trailer's editorial history is established.
* Log immutability and retention are defined.
* Anti-patterns for unlogged approvals, log editing, and evidence-free approvals are documented.
* Invariants AAL-701 through AAL-710 and ADR-1064 through ADR-1073 are established.

# Purpose

This section closes **Section 7.4 — Human Approval & Editorial Gates** and defines the transition to Section 7.5.

This section confirms the human approval layer is complete and hands forward to Part 7's acceptance and transition.

# Section 7.4 Overview

Section 7.4 established the human approval layer.

```text
7.4.1 → Purpose & Editorial Authority Boundary

```

Together these define where human judgment enters the pipeline, how it is recorded, scoped, revoked, and audited, and how it composes with the mechanical gates without ever replacing them.

---

# The Central Guarantee

Section 7.4 rests on one guarantee.

> **The human owns the substance of an approval; the machine owns only its presence, scope, and currency. Approval is additive to the mechanical gates and the non-waivable floor, never a substitute for either, and every approval decision is recorded and reconstructable.**

Every subsection of 7.4 serves this guarantee. Editorial judgment is real and load-bearing, but it operates above the mechanical floor, is bound to exact artifact identities, and is fully auditable.

---

---

An approval is a state machine bound to exactly one artifact identity.

---

Approval is an `APPROVAL_REQUIRED` dependency, scoped exactly, never widened.

---

## Invariant SAT74-705

A granted approval is revocable before irreversibility, and re-approval is always a fresh decision.

---

## Invariant SAT74-706

Approval is additive to the mechanical gates and never releases a non-waivable check.

---

## Invariant SAT74-707

Every approval action is recorded in an append-only audit log.

---

## Invariant SAT74-708

No trailer is published without a complete, logged editorial approval chain.

# 7.5 Acceptance & Transition

# Part 7.5.1 — Part 7 Architecture Summary

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section summarizes the complete **Quality Gates architecture** established across Part 7 and presents it as a single coherent system.

Sections 7.1 through 7.4 defined the gate architecture, the pipeline-stage gates, quality measurement, and human approval. This section draws them together: it states what Part 7 as a whole guarantees, how its four sections compose, and what a reader should carry forward as the essential shape of the platform's quality control.

Part 7 is large, but its architecture is simple to state: one gate model, instantiated at every boundary, measured above a mandatory floor, and topped with human judgment where consequence demands it. This section makes that shape explicit.

---

# What Part 7 Established

Part 7 defined the platform's complete quality control system.

```text
7.1  → Quality Gate Architecture     (the reusable gate model)
7.2  → Pipeline Stage Quality Gates  (gate instances at every boundary)
7.3  → Quality Metrics & Scoring     (measurement above the floor)
7.4  → Human Approval & Editorial    (human judgment above the floor)
7.5  → Acceptance & Transition       (this consolidation)
```

Section 7.1 is the foundation; 7.2, 7.3, and 7.4 build on it and never redefine it. The whole is a layered system in which each layer adds a capability strictly above the mandatory floor established by the layer beneath.

---

# The Layered Quality Model

Part 7 is a layered architecture.

```text
Layer 4  Human Approval        (7.4)  → human judgment, above the floor
Layer 3  Measurement & Scoring (7.3)  → quality measured, above the floor
Layer 2  Pipeline Stage Gates  (7.2)  → mandatory floor at every boundary
Layer 1  Gate Architecture     (7.1)  → the reusable gate model
```

Each layer is subordinate to the layer beneath.

* Layer 1 defines what a gate *is*.
* Layer 2 places mandatory gates at every stage boundary — the admissibility floor.
* Layer 3 measures quality *above* that floor and never lowers it.
* Layer 4 adds human judgment *above* that floor and never releases it.

The floor established in Layer 2 is never weakened by Layer 3 or Layer 4. Scoring adds strictness; approval adds a condition. Neither subtracts.

---

# The One Gate Model, Instantiated Everywhere

The central architectural achievement of Part 7 is a single gate model instantiated at every boundary.

```text
Section 7.1 defines:  GateDefinition, Check, Verdict, Report, Lifecycle, Governance, Config
        │
        ▼
Section 7.2 instantiates:  Story, Screenplay, Shot, Storyboard, Render,
                           Composition, Narration, Packaging, Publication, Cross-Stage
```

Every gate the platform runs is the same machine with different checks. The Shot Gate and the Publication Gate share anatomy, verdict semantics, reporting, lifecycle, and governance; they differ only in their checks and owners. This is what makes "PASS" mean the same deterministic, fail-closed, governed thing at every boundary.

---

# The Complete Control Flow

Part 7 governs the complete flow from article to released trailer.

```text
Article
  │
  ▼
Story ──[Story Gate]── Screenplay ──[Screenplay Gate]── Shot ──[Shot Gate]──
Storyboard ──[Storyboard Gate + Storyboard Approval]── Render ──[Render Gate + render↔storyboard coherence]──
Composition ──[Composition Gate]── Narration ──[Narration Gate]── Packaging ──[Packaging Gate]──
Publication ──[Publication Gate + Final/Publication Approval + non-waivable rights & safety]──
  │
  ▼
Released Trailer
```

Every arrow crosses a gate. Every gate is deterministic and fail-closed. Two boundaries additionally require human approval. Two are guarded by cross-stage coherence gates. The Publication Gate carries the non-waivable floor. Reaching the released trailer proves every one of these conditions held.

---

# The Four Pillars

Part 7 rests on four pillars, one per section.

## Uniformity (7.1)

Every gate is an instance of one architecture. Quality control is uniform, not a patchwork of per-stage inventions.

---

## Coverage (7.2)

Every stage boundary is gated, and every drift-prone transformation is checked. Coverage is complete, not selective.

---

## Measurability (7.3)

Quality is measured continuously above the floor, with deterministic scores, regression detection, and a drill-downable dashboard. Quality is observable, not merely binary.

---

## Accountability (7.4)

Human judgment enters at high-consequence boundaries, is bound to exact identities, and is fully audited. Editorial decisions are accountable, not informal.

---

# What a Reader Carries Forward

The essential shape of Part 7 is four sentences.

* A quality gate is a deterministic, fail-closed, governed checkpoint; every gate is an instance of one model (7.1).
* Every pipeline boundary is guarded by such a gate, and drift between stages is checked by cross-stage gates (7.2).
* Quality is measured above the mandatory floor, and scoring can only add strictness, never lower the floor (7.3).
* Human approval is required where cost escalates or output becomes irreversible, is identity-bound, and is fully audited (7.4).

Everything else in Part 7 elaborates these four sentences.

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant P7S-701

Part 7 defines a layered quality model in which each layer adds capability strictly above the mandatory floor.

---

## Invariant P7S-702

Section 7.1 is the foundation; 7.2, 7.3, and 7.4 instantiate and extend it without redefining it.

---

## Invariant P7S-703

Every gate the platform runs is an instance of the single Section 7.1 gate model.

---

## Invariant P7S-704

The mandatory floor established in 7.2 is never weakened by measurement (7.3) or approval (7.4).

---

## Invariant P7S-705

Every pipeline boundary is gated, and every drift-prone transformation is checked by a cross-stage gate.

---

## Invariant P7S-706

Scoring adds strictness above the floor and never subtracts from it.

---

## Invariant P7S-707

Human approval adds a condition above the floor and never releases a mandatory or non-waivable check.

---

## Invariant P7S-708

"PASS" means the same deterministic, fail-closed, governed thing at every boundary.

---

## Invariant P7S-709

Reaching the released trailer proves every gate, coherence check, approval, and non-waivable check held.

---

## Invariant P7S-710

The four pillars — uniformity, coverage, measurability, accountability — hold across the whole of Part 7.

---

# Architectural Decision Records

| ADR      | Decision                                                                            |
| -------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| ADR-1081 | Part 7 is a layered quality model with each layer above the mandatory floor.          |
| ADR-1082 | Section 7.1 is the foundation; 7.2–7.4 instantiate and extend it.                     |
| ADR-1083 | Every gate is an instance of the single Section 7.1 model.                           |
| ADR-1084 | The mandatory floor is never weakened by measurement or approval.                    |
| ADR-1085 | Every boundary is gated and every drift-prone transformation is checked.              |
| ADR-1086 | Scoring only adds strictness above the floor.                                        |
| ADR-1087 | Approval only adds a condition above the floor.                                      |
| ADR-1088 | "PASS" is uniform across all boundaries.                                             |
| ADR-1089 | Reaching the released trailer proves the whole quality chain held.                    |
| ADR-1090 | Part 7 rests on uniformity, coverage, measurability, and accountability.              |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.5.1** is complete when:

* The complete Part 7 architecture is summarized as a layered model.
* The subordination of measurement and approval to the mandatory floor is restated.
* The single-gate-model-instantiated-everywhere achievement is stated.
* The complete control flow from article to released trailer is presented.
* The four pillars — uniformity, coverage, measurability, accountability — are defined.
* The essential four-sentence shape of Part 7 is stated.
* Invariants P7S-701 through P7S-710 and ADR-1081 through ADR-1090 are established.

# Part 7.5.2 — Consolidated Invariants & Guarantees

## Insynergy Cinematic Thought Leadership Platform

### Master Specification v2.1

---

# Purpose

This section consolidates the **load-bearing invariants and guarantees** of Part 7 into a single authoritative set.

Sections 7.1 through 7.4 each established local invariants. This section gathers the ones that hold across the whole of Part 7 — the guarantees a reader, an implementer, or an auditor can rely on regardless of which gate or stage they are examining.

These consolidated guarantees are the contract Part 7 makes with the rest of the platform. Everything else in Part 7 exists to uphold them.

---

# The Ten Platform Quality Guarantees

Part 7 makes ten guarantees that hold for every gate at every boundary.

---

## Guarantee G7-01 — Determinism

Every gate verdict, score, and report is deterministic: identical artifact identity and configuration yield identical results. (7.1.5, 7.3.3)

---

## Guarantee G7-02 — Fail-Closed

Every gate defaults to FAIL: absence, ambiguity, and error resolve to FAIL, never to PASS. (7.1.5)

---

## Guarantee G7-03 — Enforcement, Not Authorship

Every gate enforces rules owned by upstream sections and authors none of its own. (7.1.1, 7.2.1)

---

## Guarantee G7-04 — Single Model

Every gate is an instance of one architecture, executed by one engine. (7.1.3, 7.5.1)

---

## Guarantee G7-05 — Complete Coverage

Every pipeline boundary is gated, and every drift-prone transformation is checked by a cross-stage gate. (7.2.1, 7.2.11)

---

## Guarantee G7-06 — Traceability

Every verdict, score, and approval is traceable to its evidence and its owner, through immutable reports and an append-only audit log. (7.1.8, 7.3.6, 7.4.6)

---

## Guarantee G7-07 — The Mandatory Floor

A mandatory check can never be rescued, lowered, or bypassed by a score, an approval, a profile, or an error. (7.3.1, 7.3.7, 7.4.1)

---

## Guarantee G7-08 — The Non-Waivable Floor

Rights and safety checks at publication can never be released by any waiver, authority, deadline, or profile. (7.1.10, 7.2.10)

---

## Guarantee G7-09 — Separation of Duties

Authoring a rule, enforcing it, waiving its failure, and approving an artifact are separated powers held by distinct authorities, every action recorded. (7.1.10, 7.4.1)

---

## Guarantee G7-10 — Immutability During a Build

Gate definitions, configurations, and thresholds are frozen for the duration of a build; the mandatory floor is profile-invariant. (7.1.11, 7.3.4)

---

# The Guarantee Dependency Structure

The ten guarantees depend on one another in a fixed structure.

```text
G7-02 (fail-closed)
   │
   ├──► G7-07 (mandatory floor)
   │        │
   │        └──► G7-08 (non-waivable floor)
   │
G7-01 (determinism)
   │
   └──► G7-06 (traceability)
   │
G7-04 (single model)
   │
   └──► G7-05 (complete coverage)
   │
G7-03 (enforce, not author)
   │
   └──► G7-09 (separation of duties)
             │
             └──► G7-10 (immutability during a build)
```

`G7-02` (fail-closed) and `G7-01` (determinism) are the roots. The floors (`G7-07`, `G7-08`) derive from fail-closed; traceability derives from determinism; coverage derives from the single model; and the governance guarantees derive from enforce-not-author. Weakening a root guarantee weakens everything that depends on it.

---

# The Guarantees Are Jointly Sufficient

The ten guarantees are jointly sufficient to establish the platform's quality claim.

> **A trailer that reaches release has passed every mandatory check at every boundary, met every required quality threshold, satisfied every cross-stage coherence check, cleared every non-waivable rights and safety check, carried every required human approval on its exact identity, and left a complete, immutable, deterministic audit trail of all of it.**

Each clause of this claim is guaranteed by one or more of the ten guarantees. Together they are the whole of what Part 7 promises.

---

# Consolidated Invariant Register

Part 7 defined the following invariant families.

| Family prefix(es)                                   | Section | Count |
| --------------------------------------------------- | ------- | ----- |
| QGB, QGP, GM, CK, FC, GT, GO, GR, GL, GG, GC, ADR7, QGS, plus the 10 stable | 7.1 | ~140 |
| SGT, SQG, SCG, SHG, SBG, RQG, CQG, NQG, MQG, PQG, XSG, SAT7 | 7.2 | ~118 |
| QMP, QMM, QSC, QTT, QRD, QDB, QEN, SAT73             | 7.3     | ~78  |
| EAB, ASM, EGT, ADS, ARR, AAL, SAT74                 | 7.4     | ~68  |
| P7S, P7G, P7T, P7A, P7X                              | 7.5     | ~40  |

Every local invariant serves one or more of the ten consolidated guarantees. The guarantees are the summary; the local invariants are the detailed obligations that uphold them.

---

# Architectural Invariants

## Invariant P7G-701

The ten platform quality guarantees hold for every gate at every boundary.

---

## Invariant P7G-702

Every local invariant in Part 7 serves one or more of the ten consolidated guarantees.

---

## Invariant P7G-703

Fail-closed and determinism are the root guarantees from which the others derive.

---

## Invariant P7G-704

The mandatory floor and the non-waivable floor are never weakened by any other guarantee.

---

## Invariant P7G-705

The ten guarantees are jointly sufficient to establish the platform's quality claim.

---

## Invariant P7G-706

Traceability holds across verdicts, scores, and approvals through immutable records.

---

## Invariant P7G-707

Separation of duties holds across authoring, enforcing, waiving, and approving.

---

## Invariant P7G-708

Immutability during a build holds for definitions, configuration, and thresholds.

---

## Invariant P7G-709

Weakening a root guarantee weakens everything that depends on it.

---

## Invariant P7G-710

The consolidated guarantees are the contract Part 7 makes with the rest of the platform.

---

| ADR-1098 | Immutability during a build spans definitions, configuration, and thresholds.         |
| ADR-1099 | Weakening a root guarantee weakens its dependents.                                   |
| ADR-1100 | The consolidated guarantees are Part 7's contract with the platform.                 |

---

# Acceptance Criteria

Section **7.5.2** is complete when:

* The ten platform quality guarantees are defined and traced to their sections.
* The guarantee dependency structure is presented with fail-closed and determinism as roots.
* The joint sufficiency of the guarantees for the platform's quality claim is stated.
